CURVATURE LIMITATIONS IN BRIDGE CODES

Imad Eldin Ahmed Eisa Khalafalla B.Sc. Civil Engineering, University of Khartoum

111

"Curvature Limitations in Bridge Codes"
By !mad Eldin Ahmed Eisa Khalafalla Ryerson University- Civil Engineering Toronto, Ontario, Canada, 2009

ABSTRACT
In recent years, horizontally curved bridges have been widely used in congested urban areas, where multi- level interchange structures are necessary for modem highways. A series of horizontally curved bridges were analyzed in this study using a commercial finite-element program, ABAQUS. In each analysis, the behavior of bridges was investigated, and the major internal forces developed in the members were determined. Specifically, stresses, vertical deflections, vertical reactions and natural frequencies for different values of the horizontal angle of curvature. These values compared with the values of a straight bridge of similar span and cross-section configuration. Bridges taken into account were single and twospan horizontally curved concrete slab bridges, concrete T-beam bridges, concrete cellular bridges, concrete box girder bridges, slab-on-steel !-girder bridges, and composite steel box girder bridges. The design parameters considered were degree of curvature, span length, number of lanes, number of girders and/or boxes, and span-to-depth ratio. The stipulation made in bridge codes for treating a curved bridge as straight one is examined. Based on the data generated from the parametric study, sets of empirical expressions were developed to evaluate stress, deflection, reaction, and frequency distribution factors in a curved bridge system as related to a straight bridge system. Then these expressions were extended to establish more reliable expressions for curvature limitation to treat a curved bridge as straight one.

IV

CHAPTER I INTRODUCTION
1.1 General
In the past, highway bridges were placed by determining the most suitable crossing site, with slight regard to the general alignment of the roadway. After the bridge was located, the highway designer or surveyor laid out the highway to meet the bridge. In recent years, this situation has reversed and now bridges must fit the highway alignment that has been prearranged according to certain considerations. The growing demands on structures of curved alignment is presenting real challenges to engineers, especially in the design of congested urban areas where multi-level interchanges must be built within inflexible geometric restrictions. The use of horizontally curved bridges in a structure on curved alignment is a natural outcome of this trend toward aesthetic design. Beside the aesthetic considerations, horizontally curved bridges offer certain technical advantages where structures must be built to fit curved highway alignment, when compared with bridges composed of straight girders segments. In addition, horizontally curved bridges allow the designer to make use of continuous construction and its inherent advantages in situations which might otherwise be limited to simple spans. Continuous spans make more efficient use of materials and allow the elimination of many unwanted expansion details. A stiffer structure is obtained and in some cases more vertical clearance is available due to the use of shallower bridges. Horizontally curved girders also allow the designer increased flexibility, where possible locations of substructures are often limited because of required clearances. The use of straight bridges to span the same distance could mean a complicated framing system to _
t

1

support the deck. When high substructures are involved, the use of longer spans may also result tn savtngs. Nowadays, horizontally curved bridges became an important component in highway bridges and the major advantages are structural efficiency, appearance, smooth traffic flow, and simplicity in detailing, and construction.

1.2 The Problem
In the early days of curved bridge design and construction, bridge superstructures supporting curved roadway alignment were comprised of short straight girders linked together at the supports. This resulted in inefficient use of very short spans between support piers. As the technology for designing and fabricating curved girders became available, it became possible to design curved bridges with much greater distances between supports. Most recently, curved girders are widely used in bridge superstructures. However, due to the addition of curvature, the design and construction of bridges becomes greatly more complicated than that of straight bridges, and their structural behavior still not well understood. While in straight bridges, the girders, stringers, and floor beams can be designed by systematically isolating each member and applying standard loads, curved bridges must be designed with careful consideration to system-wide behavior. In essence, the addition of curvature adds torsion to the system that results in significant warping and distortional stresses within the member cross-sections. Furthermore, "secondary members" such as cross frames and diaphragms that provide stability in straight bridges become primary load carrying members in curved bridges. Current codes pertaining to analysis and design of horizontally curved bridges are mostly based on experimental and analytical research conducted over 30 years ago as part of project CURT (Consortium ofUniversity Research Teams, 1975).

2

Treating the horizontally curved bridges as straight one with certain limitations is one of the methods to simplify the analysis and design procedure. Recently the CHBDC (2006), the AASHTO Guide Specifications for Horizontally Curved Bridges, (Guide, 2003), and AASHTOLRFD (AASHTO, 2007) have specified certain limitations to treat horizontally curved bridges as straight one, but still more investigations are needed to examine these limitations for different bridges configurations, by considering new parameters, developing empirical expressions for stress, reaction, deflection, and frequency distribution factors to assist in filling the gaps found in the bridge codes. CHBDC and the AASHTO Guide Specifications for Horizontally Curved Bridges have recommended the use of finite-element methods (FEA) as one of the methods for analysis for horizontally curved bridges, and it was used in this study. This study is an attempt to realize the abovementioned need. The results and the equations provided in this study will help bridge engineers in predicting new and more reliable limitations in treating the horizontally curved bridges as straight ones.

1.3 Objectives
The overall objectives of the research reported herein are: 1. Examine the applicability of the specified limitations of bridge curvature in North American Bridge Codes to treat a horizontally curved bridge as straight one in structural analysis and design. 2. Providing information that complements existing data and that will be practical for formulating complete design procedure for horizontally curved bridges. 3. Developing empirical expressions for stress, reaction, deflection, and frequency distribution factors to treat horizontally curved bridge as straight one.
"

3

1.4 Scope
The scope of this study includes the following: 1. A literature review on limitations in CHBDC and AASHTO codes related to the study. 2. Conducting a parametric study to examtne bridge curvature limitations. The parameters to be considered in the study: degree of curvature, span length, numbers of lanes, and number of girders and/or boxes. 3. Constructing a finite element models using ABAQUS software to analyze these bridges under dead load to obtain bending stresses, reactions, vertical deflections, and natural frequencies for each studied bridge. 4. Analyzing the results, and make recommendations for modification of code equation, if applicable.

1.5 Contents and Arrangement of this study
Chapter II contains the literature review on types of bridges covered in this study, bridge curvature limitations in North American Codes (CHBDC, 2000), American bridge codes (AASHTO-LRFD, 2007; AASHTO, Guide, 1993, and 2003: ACI Manual of Concrete Practice Part 3, 2008), and previous studies related to distribution factors for straight and curved bridges. Chapter III describes the finite-element method, computer software "ABAQUS" used in the analysis, and modeling of concrete and steel bridges. Chapter IV presents the result of the parametric study performed in this study, and developed equations for bridge curvature limitations. Chapter V is the summary, conclusions, and recommendations for further future studies.

4

CHAPTER II LITERATURE REVIEW

2.1 Horizontally Curved Concrete Bridge Types
In the bridge superstructures reinforced concrete sections are usually consist of slabs, Tbeams, and box girders (Figure 2.1 ). Generally the controlling factors in the selection of the proper types of bridges are
cost~effectiveness,

and aesthetics. Occasionally, the selection is

complicated by other considerations such as the deflection limits, life-cycle cost, traffic maintenance during construction stages, construction scheduling and worker safety, passage of flood debris, seismicity at the site, suitability for future widening, and commitments made to officials and individuals of the community. In some cases, a prestressed concrete or steel bridge may be a better choice. 2.1.1 Slab Bridges The reinforced concrete slab bridges have the simplest superstructure configuration and the neatest appearance. They usually require more reinforcing steel and structural concrete than do girder-type bridges of the same span; However, the design details and formworks are easier and less expensive, and the construction time is shortest of any cast-in-place bridges. It has been found economical for simply supported span up to 9 m and for continuous spans up to 12 m. The maintenance of these types of bridges is very little except at transverse deck joints, when used, and bearing usually require little maintenance.

5

2.1.2 T-Beam Bridges The T-Beam bridge consists of a transversely reinforced slab deck which spans across to the longitudinal support girders. These require a more complex formwork, particularly for skewed bridges, compared to the other superstructure forms. Generally the T-Beam bridges are more economical for spans of 12 to 18m. The girder stem thickness usually varies from 35 to 55 em and is controlled by the required horizontal spacing of the positive moment reinforcement. Best lateral spacing of longitudinal girders is typically between 1.8 to 3.0 m for a minimum cost of formwork and structural materials. However, where vertical supports for the formwork are difficult and expensive, girder spacing can be increased accordingly. Utilities, pipes, and conduit can be concealed between girders. Usually the construction time of these types of bridges is somewhat longer than required for slab bridges due to more complex forming. Their maintenance is low, except that bearing and transverse deck joint details may require attention. At stream crossing, with inadequate underclearance, floating may damage the girder stem. 2.1.3 Box-Girder Bridges The Box-Girder Bridges have top deck, vertical web, bottom slab and are often used for spans of 15 to 36 m with girder spaced at 1.5 m times the structure depth. Outside this range, it is probably more economical to consider a different type of bridge, such as post-tensioned box girder or steel girder superstructure. This is because of the massive increase in volume and materials. The high torsional strength of the box girder makes it particularly suitable for sharp curve alignment, skewed piers and abutment, superelevation, and transitions such as interchange ramp structures. Utilities, pipes, and conduit can be concealed between boxes. It needs more construction time compared with slab or T-Beam bridges. Their maintenance is low, except that bearing and transverse deck joint details may cause some problems.

6

2.2 Horizontally Curved Steel Bridge Types
Horizontally curved steel bridges are used extensively in the construction of urban highways, and are continue to occupy a rising share of the bridge market. These structures, which constituted approximately a quarter of the steel bridges being constructed in the early 1990s [Structural Stability Research Council (SSRC) 1991 ], are often one of the few practical options at complicated interchanges or river crossings where limited site space or pier locations are available. Horizontally curved steel bridges also offer aesthetic and cost benefits over more traditional chorded structures that make their selection attractive even when site restrictions are not an issue. They are shallower than curved concrete bridges, which can often result in shorter approaches or fewer retaining walls when compared to structures containing deeper girders. Horizontally curved steel bridges can also be skewed to meet site demands. Given the benefits realized with the design and construction of horizontally curved steel bridges (e.g., reduced number of substructure units and length of deck overhangs; increased spans and traffic sight distances) and the continued decrease in available land space for new and replacement structures, the use of these bridge types is certain to increase. However, there is considerable additional complication associated with their analysis, design and construction compared to that for typical straight bridges. There are two general types of horizontally curved steel bridges, Steel-Concrete Composite !-Girder Bridges, and Steel-Concrete Composite Box Girder Bridges.

2.2.1 Steel-Concrete Composite 1-Girder Bridges
Steel !-girders are perhaps more commonly used for horizontally curved bridges. These members have very little torsional stiffness and are stable only when connected to other girders using cross frames or diaphragms. Curvature of the superstructure leads to combined bending and torsion in the girders, significant forces in diaphragm and bracing members, and considerably more

7

interaction between components within the structural system than experienced in straight bridges. These effects must be considered in the analysis and design to ensure that the bridge components are proportioned properly. Curved steel I-girder bridges are more attractive choice for most designers due to the following reasons: simplicity of fabrication and construction, less land is needed during erection, shallower sections can be designed, lighter weight of steel girders, and excellent serviceability performance. Further more, curved I-girder bridges also provide a very appealing aesthetic shape than the chorded structure and thus are more attractive to conform to the existing terrain.

2.2.2 Steel-Concrete Composite Box Girder Bridges
Box girders are used extensively in the construction of urban highways, horizontally curved, and long-span bridges. Box girders have higher flexural capacity and torsional rigidity, and the closed shape reduces the exposed surface, making them less susceptible to corrosion. Box girders also provide smooth, aesthetically pleasing structures. There are two types of steel box girders: steel-concrete composite box girders (i.e., steel box composite with concrete deck) and steel box girders with orthotropic decks. Composite box girders are generally used in moderate- to medium-span (30 to 60 m) bridges, and steel box girders with orthotropic decks are often used for longer-span bridges. Composite box-girder bridges usually have single or multiple boxes. A single cell box is easy to analyze and relies on torsional stiffness to carry eccentric loads. The required flexural stiffness is independent of the torsional stiffness. A single box girder with multiple cells is economical for very long spans. Multiple webs reduce the flange shear lag and also share the shear forces. The bottom flange creates more equal deformations and better load distribution between adjacent

8

girders. The boxes in multiple box girders are relatively small and close together, making the flexural and torsional stiffness usually very high. The torsional stiffness of the individual boxes is generally less important than its relative flexural stiffness.

2.3 Limitations for Neglecting Curvature Effect in Horizontally Curved Bridges
Recently the Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code CHBDC (2000) and the AASHTO Guide Specifications for Horizontally Curved Bridges, (Guide 1993 & 2003) have specified certain limitations to treat horizontally curved bridges as straight one. These limitations can be summarized as follows:

2.3.1

Guide Specifications for Horizontally Curved Highway Bridges (AASHTO 1993)

For the Curved 1-Girder bridges, the effects of curvature may be neglected in determining the primary bending moments in the longitudinal members, when the central angle subtended by each span is less the values given in Table 2.1.
Table 2.1 Limiting central angle for neglecting curvature in determining primary bending moment

Numbers of Girders 2 3 or4 5 or more

Angle for one Span 20 30 40

Angle for two or More Spans 30 40 50

9

2.3.2

Guide Specifications for Horizontally Curved Steel Girder Highway Bridges (AASHTO 2003)

2.3.2.1 !-Girders
In the !-girder bridges, the effect of curvature may be ignored in the determination of vertic,Y moment, when the following three conditions are met: o Girders are concentric.

o Bearing lines are not skewed more than 10 degrees from radial, and o The arc span divided by the girder radius is less than 0.06 radians.

2.3.2.2 Closed box and Tub Girders
In the box-girder bridges, the effect of curvature may be ignored in the determination of vertical moment, when the following three conditions are met: o Girders are concentric.

o Bearing are not skewed, and o The arc span divided by the girder radius is less than 0.3 radians and the girder depth is less than the width of the box at mid-depth.

2.3.3 AASHTO-LRFD Bridge Design Specifications (2004) 2.3.3.1 Concrete Box Girders
Except for cast-in-place multicell concrete box girders, segments of horizontally curved superstructures with torsionally stiff closed or portion thereof is less than 12 degrees may be analyzed as if the segment were straight. Horizontally cast-in-place multicell box girders may be designed as single-spine beams with straight segments, for central angle up to 34 degrees within one span, unless concerns about other force effects dictate otherwise.

2.4 Review of Previous Study on Curved Bridges
2.4.1 General Review of Study
A few design and construction of horizontally curved steel bridges occurred, prior to 1960s.

Curved steel girders were utilized only if using a chorded structure proved to be unworkable, and there were no any guidelines or specifications used to in designing these types of girders. Regardless of the lack of specifications, engineers began to identify the advantages related to the curved structures, and horizontally curved bridges were being designed with increasing regularity in the 1960s. The need to ensure uniform minimum standards of practice and safety for this structure type led directly to efforts to develop design guidelines that would put curved girder bridges on the same footing as more traditional highway bridges designed according to AASHTO Specifications. However, this need led to creation of the Consortium of University Research Teams (CURT) project, a large-scale research project funded by 25 states and managed by the Federal Highway Administration (FHWA), in 1969. The main goals of this project were: 11

12-

Reviewing all existing publications on curved bridges; Conducting experimental and analytical research to increase existing information related to curved girders;

34-

Incorporating research results from ongoing state agency sponsored projects; Develop simplified analysis and design methods with accompanying aids and computer programs; and

5-

Correlated proposed analysis and design methods and procedures with existing analytical and experimental data.

A series of scale model laboratory tests accompanied by theoretical work and analytical studies, were created as a result from the CURT project. (Mozer and Culver 1970; Mozer et al. 1971, 1973; Brennan 1970, 1971, 1974) conducted series of tests on !-girders to examine the behavior of single girders and girder pairs, and they studied the interaction between adjacent curved girders and the effect of bracing members in that interaction. Also one of the outputs of these studies, was a proposed three-dimensional analysis method and related computer code As a result of the CURT project, a lot of theoretical and analytical works were completed, and developments of empirical models were conducted. These studies can be summarized as follows: (1) (McManus 1971), overall strength of doubly symmetric curved girder I sections in bending; (2) (Nasir 1970), local buckling behavior of curved girder flanges; and (3) (Brogan 1974; Culver et al. 1972, 1973), behavior of web panels in flexure. From these experimental and mathematical studies, a tentative set of Specifications for allowable stress design (ASD) of curved girder bridges were developed (Culver 1972; CURT 1975). The proposed Specifications were reviewed by the Task Committee on Curved Girders of the ASCE-

12

AASHTO Committee on Flexural Members, and they also considered further curved bridge experimental and analytical studies. These studies were completed at the same time as the CURT project (Heins 1972; Mondkar and Powell 1974), and this work was combined with the CURT specifications and proposed by the Task Committee as guide specifications that were accepted by AASHTO in 1976 (Armstrong 1977). In the mid 1970s, the load factor design (LFD) criteria were added to the guide specifications through a research project sponsored by the American Iron and Steel Institute (AISI) (Stegmann and Galambos 1976), and this project changed the ASD criteria proposed by the CURT project team to an LFD format similar to that available in the 1973 AASHTO Standard Specifications for Highway Bridges (AASHTO 1973). The LFD criteria were developed directly from the proposed CURT specifications, and they were adopted by AASHTO and added to the ASD criteria to form the first edition of the Guide Specs (AASHTO 1980). A second edition from the Guide Specs was published in 1993, after a number of revisions have been published, but the document issued in 1980 is largely unchanged in its fundamental content The research in the behavior of horizontally curved steel bridges continued after initial publication of the Guide Specs. (e.g., Yoo and Carbine 1985). In the early 1990s an intensive efforts were initiated to improve these specifications. Specifically, after Task Group 14 of the Structural Stability Research Council on Horizontally Curved Girders published a report outlining problems associated with the specifications in their current form and proposed areas for research (SSRC 1991). However, this report provided the impulsion for a number of research projects aimed at developing updated and improved curved girder design specifications. In 1992 the Curved Steel Bridge Research Project (CSBRP), was initiated by FHWA. The objectives of this project were:

13

1-

Collection and distribution of all curved bridge research completed in the U.S. and

abroad; and 2Experimentally and analytically address the behavior of curved I girders in bending,

shear, and combined bending and shear. This project has performed an extensive number of computer simulations using sophisticated finite element models, and a number of publications have been produced (Zurieck et al. 1994; Linzell1999; Zureick et al. 2000; Jung and White 2001; Zureick et al. 2001 ). Another project was initiated in 1993, sponsored by the National Cooperative Highway Research Program (NCHRP), to develop a set of improved specifications. The results of this project are published in NCHRP Report 424 (Hallet al. 1999). This report includes: a summary of past and ongoing curved bridge research, discussions of current U.S. curved bridge design and construction practices, an overview of the Recommended Specs (Hall and Yoo 1998) for LFD and construction of curved I and box girders, and future research recommendations. In 1999, a new project was initiated jointly by AISI and FHWA to extend the results of experimental tests conducted under the CURT project and the CSBRP through refined nonlinear finite element analysis, and to develop equations for load and resistance factor design of curved and straight steel bridge girders. The findings of this project contain: a general review of

curved !-girder maximum strength equations that have been proposed within the literature, a recommended set of modifications to the AASHTO LRFD Bridge Design Specifications (AASHTO 2001 ), and correlation of the recommended design equations with prior experimental results and analytical results from the parametric study.

14

Another project research was initiated by NCHRP Project 12-52. The main objective of this project was to revise and recalibrate the 2003 Guide Specs so that LRFD can be applied to curved steel bridges.

2.4.2 Other Research Studies
In 1970, Heins and Siminou initiated a research to study a simplified method for evaluating the

internal forces and deformations in radial curved girder system. They developed equations, and distribution factors that will help in determination of required cross-sectional properties in a single, two, and three spans curved girder system. The developed design equations in this study, have the following limitations: (1) girder spacing between 2 to 3m; (2) girder span lengths varied from 15 to 30m; (3) girders of the system must have a constant curvature limited to radii between 30 to 180 m; the system may be 4, 6

15

In their study, Heins and Siminou's introduced design charts for modification factors of moment, shear, deflection, rotation, and warping torsion.

In 1984 Heins and Jin studied the effect of cross bracing spacing on curved bridge distribution
factors. This study covered both single-girder and multi-girder system. They developed a space model frame idealization and used grid analysis for obtaining their results. They produced number of graphs and equations that correlate the effect of the cross bracing and distribution factor.

In 1985, Yoo and Littrell used 3-D finite-element method (SAP), to study the response of a
system of 5 girders horizontally curved, connected by a slab and cross bracing under dead load and live load. The parameters considered in this study were: radius of curvature, length of girders and number of braced intervals. They developed empirical design equations to predict the ratio of: (a) Maximum bending stress; (b) maximum warping stress; and (c) maximum deck deflection. They concluded that: ( 1) maximum bending stresses and maximum deck deflections stabilized with minimal bracing; (2) warping stresses were sensitive to the number of braced intervals. They also observed that partially loaded lanes; 2 trucks out of 3 trucks (deck width capacity) located near the outside edge of the bridge produced higher stresses and deflection in the curved bridges due to the tilting of the bridge deck created by the nonsymmetrical load distribution.

In 1986, Brockenbrough used a 3-D finite-element model to study the effect of various
parameters on the load distribution for 4-girder curved bridge. A two-span bridge comprised of composite concrete deck with steel !-girder and intermediate transverse cross frames between girders was investigated in this study. The conclusions of this study were: (1) the central angle per span, which includes the combined effect of curvature and span length had larger effects, (2)

16

the girder spacing, had larger effects identified; (3) variation in girder stiffness and cross-frame spacing had relatively small effects on live-load distribution factors. In this research Brockenbrough developed charts showing the variation of the distribution factors with the variation of these parameters.

In 1989, Schelling and Namini presented the results of a comprehensive research to study the
response of simple and continuous span horizontally curved steel I-girder bridges with and without cross bracing, during construction phase due to self own weight. Based on 3-D space frame model, Schelling and Namini developed empirical equations of moment distribution factors for different numbers of girders (2, 4, & 6) to determine the effect of the lateral bracing on curved bridges during construction. In their research, they concluded that the results from the simple-span can be applied conservatively to the continuous span bridges provided that the supports and radial span length ratios do not differ greatly from unity. Their developed empirical equation had a drawback because it can be used in conjunction with the results given by the 2-dimentional grid analysis method. In addition to that the range of the bridges spans considered in their models were (36m to 90m) which are impractical for slab on
'

steel I-girder bridges.

Davidson et al. (1996) performed a research to study certain design parameters affecting the
behavior of horizontally curved steel I-girder bridges. A finite-element, ABAQUS software was used in this study to construct a 3-girder Bridge model. The parameters considered in this study were: cross-frame spacing, span length, girder depth, number of girders, flange width, girder spacing. This study concluded that the span length, radius of curvature, flange width and cross frame spacing have the greatest effect on the warping-to-bending stress ratio. Based on this information, a regression analysis was performed to predict the effect of these parameters on the

17

warping-to-bending stress ratio. Consequently, an equation was developed from this regression and proposed for the preliminary cross-frame spacing design:

Where Smax is the maximum bracing spacing in meters, L is the span length in meters, R is the radius of curvature in meters, and br is the flange width in millimeters. In 2002, Zhang conducted a finite-element analysis to investigate the load distribution factors for curved !-girder bridges, in which 111 bridge models with radius of curvature less than 450m were analyzed. The parameters considered in the study were: radius of curvature (45 to 450 m), girder spacing (1.8 to 5.0 m), span length (15 to, 70 m), slab thickness (170 to 300 mm), longitudinal stiffness (32122 to 72226 cm4), number of girders (3 to 7), and cross frame spacing (2 to 7 m). In this study, Zhang concluded that radius of curvature, girder spacing, and number of girders had significant effect on the load distribution, and span length, slab thickness, and longitudinal stiffness had slight effect. Also he showed that the effect of cross frame spacing and girder torsional inertia could be neglected, and the shear distribution factors have similar trend as that of moment distribution factors. The difference is that the shear distribution factor of the outside exterior girder and the inside exterior girder are close. A number of simplified equations were developed in this study for moment (+ ve & - ve) and shear distribution factors for exterior and interior girders, and it was found that the distribution factors of the outside exterior girder positive moment obtained from AASHTO Guide Commentary method (1993) for multiple-lane loading were less conservative compared with the results of FEM analysis. Also AASHTOLRFD formulas for straight bridges led to either larger or smaller results when curved used for bridges, and Heins and Jin's formula was too conservative for all cases.

18

In 2004, W assef performed an extensive research to study the structural response of composite steel I-girder bridge. He used finite-element computer software SAP2000 to model 192 simply supported straight and curved I-girder bridges. The parameters considered in this study were: span length, girder spacing, number of girders, and span-to-radius ratio L/R. In his study, Wassef examined the influence of these parameters on the moment, deflection, and warping stress distribution, and he concluded that: ( 1) curvature is the most critical parameter that influence the design of curved bridges; (2) span length, number of girders, and girder spacing affect the values of the moment and deflection distribution factors in general; (3) Full bridge live loading does not necessary produce the extreme design values of the moment or deflection distribution factors; (4) warping-to-bending stress ratio values were acceptable and within the limits, except for bridge with L/R ratio 0.7 and span length 35m; (5) CHBDC moment and deflection distribution factors overestimate the structural response of straight bridges. In 2004, Nasr studied the live load distribution on the curved steel !-girder bridges. He performed an extensive parametric study, using the finite-element method, to determine the lateral load distribution factors based on AASHTO live loads. The parameters considered in this study were: radius of curvature, girder spacing, span length, slab thickness, number of girders, cross frame spacing, overhang width and girder longitudinal and torsional stiffness. The study findings were: (1) The distribution factors of outside exterior girder positive moment obtained from AASHTO (1993) Guide Commentary were un-conservative in some cases and conservative in other cases; (2) The outside exterior girder has always the most maximum value of moment and two-lane loading would generally produce the maximum girder response; and (3) For different bridge width the following parameters, has a significant effect on the distribution

19

factors: radius of curvature, span length, girder spacing and distance from the centre of exterior girder to the inside edge of traffic barrier.

In 2005, Al-Hashimy initiated an extensive parametric study on different simply supported ·
straight and curved slab-on-steel I-girder bridges to study their structural behaviour and response. He used a finite-element SAP200, software to model and analyse 320 prototype straight and curved bridges. The parameters considered in this study were: Span length, Girder spacing, Number of girders, and Span-to-radius ratio (L/R). The bridge prototype models were subjected to the Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code (CHBDC) CL-625 truck, lane loading and dead loading, to examine the influence of these parameters on moment, shear, deflection and warping stress distribution. Based on this study Al-Hashimy concluded that: 1. Curvature is the most critical parameter in determining the lateral distribution factors and warping-to-bending stress ratio. 2. In general, the increase in the number of girders, as well as in girders spacing results in an increase in the shear distribution factor, moment distribution factor, and deflection distribution factor. 3. Span length has slightly effect on shear distribution factors; 4. There is a small effect on the shear distribution factors when The number of crossbracing intervals are over 3,

5. The CHBDC significantly underestimates the structural response of curved bridges by
treating them as straight bridges when,

In 2008, Ihsan-ul Haq conducted an extensive parametric study, ustng the finite-element
computer software SAP2000, in which 614 simply supported curved 1-steel Girder Bridge prototypes were analyzed to examine the key parameters affecting warping stresses in curved Igirder bridges under construction loads. The parameters considered in this study were: Span length, Girder spacing, Number of girders, number of cross bracing intervals between support lines, and Span-to-radius of curvature ratio (L/R). In this study, the previously available empirical equations for minimum number of cross-bracing intervals to limit warping stresses in curved !-girders were examined. Ihsan-ul Haq also investigated the moment and shear distribution factors of each girder under construction loads. Based on this study empirical expressions for moment and shear distribution factors for the curved girder were developed. The study findings were: ( 1) The increase of number of girders for the same girder spacing decreases the warping effects; (2) Warping-to-bending stress ratio in girder flanges increases with increase in bridge curvature, and decreases with increase in flange warping constant; (3) Utilizing "Torsion Box" in the form of added horizontal bracing members joining girders' top and bottom flanges enhances the load distribution characteristics of the curved system but it has insignificant effect on the flange warping-to-bending stress ratio; and (4) Span length, radius of curvature, number of girders, and girder spacing have significant effect on the longitudinal bending moments, deflection, axial force in bracing members, and shear forces.

CHAPTER III FINITE-ELEMENT ANALYSIS

3.1 General
Preceding to the construction of any structural system, an extensive engineering design and analysis process must be undertaken. During this process, many engineering assumptions are routinely used in the application of engineering principles and theories to practice. A subset of these assumptions is used in a multitude of analytical methods available to structural analyst. In the modem engineering office, with the increased power of personal computers, increasing numbers of engineers depend on structural analysis computer software to solve their engineering problems. This modernization of the engineering design office, couples with an increased demand placed on the accuracy and efficiency of structural design, require a more-detailed understanding of the basic principles and assumptions associated with the use of modem structural analysis computer programs. The most popular of these programs are GT STRUDL, STAADIII, SAP2000, as well as some more powerful and complex tools such as ADINA, ANSYS, NASTRAN, and ABAQUS. Typically during the analytical stage of any bridge design, finite-element-based structural analysis programs are used to evaluate the structural integrity of the bridges system. Most structural analysis programs employ sound, well-established finite-element methodologies and algorithms to solve the analytical problem. It is of greatest importance for the users of these programs to understand the theories, assumptions, and limitations of numerical modeling using the finite-element method, as well as the limitations on the accuracy of computer systems used to execute these programs. The finite element method is one of the methods recognized by the

22

Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code (CHBDC 2000), section 5.9, for short and medium span bridges, and it is considered to be the most powerful, and multipurpose method. The most important advantages of the finite element method are: 1- Eliminate the differential equation completely from the solution. 2- Possibility to analyze structures having irregular shape with any material variations thereof. 3- Allow multiple loading conditions to be applied to the system. 4- Capability to use multiple structural elements such as plates, beams, and shells, 5- Ability to use multiple materials within the structure such as isotropic, orthotropic, and general anisotropic. 6- Possibility to increase the prediction accuracy in important region and reduce it in other region (thus reducing cost of the simulation). This chapter presents a brief description of finite-element approach, description of modeling methodology, description of the structural geometry, and description of the boundary conditions and loads acting on the structure. The available commercial finite-element program, ABAQUS 6.6, was used to investigate the most probable responses of a modeled bridge structure. A general description of this software is presented later in this chapter. The procedure to perform an extensive parametric study on selected straight and curved bridge prototypes, and different bridge configurations, to evaluate the stresses, vertical deflections, vertical reactions and natural frequencies for different values of degree of curvature.

3.2 Finite-Element Approach
Finite Element Analysis (FEA) is a numerical method which provides solutions to problems that would otherwise be difficult to obtain. The method has wide application and enjoys extensive

23

utilization in the structural, thermal and fluid analysis areas. The finite element method ts comprised of three major steps: (1) Pre-processing: The user constructs a model of the part to be analyzed in which the geometry is divided into a number of discrete subregions, or "elements," connected at discrete points called "nodes." Certain of these nodes will have fixed displacements, and others will have prescribed loads. For a two-dimensional (2D) analysis, or a three-dimensional (3D) thin shell analysis, the elements are essentially 2D, but may be "warped" slightly to conform to a 3D surface. For a 3D solid analysis, the elements have physical thickness in all three dimensions. In addition, there are many special elements, such as axisymmetric elements for situations in which the geometry, material and boundary conditions are all symmetric about an axis. The model's degrees of freedom (do f) are assigned at the nodes. These models can be extremely time consuming to prepare, and commercial codes vie with one another to have the most user-friendly graphical "preprocessor" to assist in this rather tedious job. Some of these preprocessors can overlay a mesh on a preexisting CAD file, so that finite element analysis can be done conveniently as part of the computerized drafting-and-design process. (2) Analysis: In this step the dataset prepared by the preprocessor is used as input to the finite element code itself, which constructs and solves a system of linear or nonlinear algebraic equations (3.1) Where:
f
K

the nodal load vector; the global stiffness matrix; the nodal displacement vector;

u

24

The formation of the K matrix is dependent on the type of problem being attacked. Commercial codes may have very large element libraries, with elements appropriate to a wide range of problem types. One of FEA's principal advantages is that many problem types can be addressed with the same code, simply by specifying the appropriate element types from the library. (3) Post-processing: In the earlier days of finite element analysis, the user would pore through reams of numbers generated by the code, listing displacements and stresses at discrete positions within the model. It is easy to miss important trends and hot spots this way, and recent codes use graphical displays to assist in visualizing the results. A typical postprocessor display overlay colored contours representing stress levels on the model, showing a full-field picture.

3.3 The Finite Element Program ABAQUS
ABAQUS is a high-performance software package developed by Hibbitt, Karlsson & Sorensen, Inc. for the numerical modeling of structural response. It enables you to do linear or nonlinear, and static or dynamic types of analysis for a large spectrum of engineering problems. The ABAQUS suite of engineering analysis software packages are used throughout the world to simulate the physical response of structures and solid bodies to load, temperature, contact, impact, and other environmental conditions. Some of the specific capabilities of ABAQUS include: stress analysis (both static and dynamic responses), dynamic studies (linear and nonlinear problems), ability to model very large shape changes in solids (both two and three dimensions), heat transfer problems, coupled heat transfer and stress analysis, coupled pore fluid diffusion and stress analysis problems, eigenvalue buckling prediction, natural frequency

25

extraction, geostatic stress state, dynamic analysis of linear systems by modal methods, and element removal and replacement. Furthermore, ABAQUS has a very extensive element library, including a full set of continuum elements, beam elements, shell and plate elements, among others. Also this software has an advanced material library, including the usual elastic and elastic - plastic solids; models for foams, concrete, soils, piezoelectric materials, and many others. A data deck for ABAQUS contains model data and history data. The model data define a finite element model (geometry, materials definition, element properties, etc.). The history data define the sequence of events or loadings. History data is divided into steps, each step being a period of response of a particular type. All data definitions in ABAQUS are accomplished with option blocks. Each option is introduced by a keyword card. Within certain restrictions, you can use user subroutines as powerful and flexible tools for analysis. Post processing options include time history plots, variable-variable plots, and printed tables showing values as functions of time, output in a neutral plot format that you can read with the post-processing routine of your choice. There are also options that allow additional output (printed tables, contour or deformed configurations plots, FILE output) to be created at specified increments from an ABAQUS restart file.

3.7 Finite-Element Bridges modeling
For more complex structures with complicated geometric configurations, such as curved girder bridges, or bridges with highly skewed supports, more-detailed finite-element models should be considered. In this study the modeling was performed using 3-D finite-element method with the ABAQUS 6.6 software to obtain, stresses, vertical deflections, vertical reactions and natural frequencies for the above mentioned concrete, and steel composite bridges and to determine their structural response. To simplify the analysis, the structure was divided into a number of components as follows: For concrete structures, the bridges were divided into a concrete deck slab, concrete webs, concrete bottom flange, and concrete solid end & intermediate diaphragms. For the steel composite structures, the bridges were divided into a concrete deck slab, steel webs, steel bottom flange, steel top flanges, steel solid end-diaphragm, cross bracing and top chords.
3.7.1 Aspect Ratio

An important issue to be considered in finite-element modeling is to improve accuracy, which in

tum results in more efficient and cost-effective design. The aspect ratio is defined as the ratio of the longest dimension to the shortest dimension of a quadrilateral element. In many cases, as the aspect ratio increases, the inaccuracy of the solution increases (Logan, 2002). Logan presented a graph showing that as the aspect ratio rises above 4, the percentage of error from the exact solution increases greater than 15%. Accordingly, in this study, the aspect ratio is kept in acceptable ranges, by considering 72 elements in the longitudinal direction for single-span

32

bridges, and 36 elements for two-span bridges, whereas 6 elements were considered in the vertical direction for the concrete bridge webs, and 4 elements for the composite bridge webs.

3. 7.2 Geometric Modeling 3.7.2.1 Concrete Bridges Modeling

From the element available in the ABAQUS library, a four-node shell element, type S4R, with six degrees of freedom at each node was selected to model the concrete deck slab, concrete webs, concrete bottom flange, and concrete solid end & intermediate diaphragms. A detailed diagram of the shell element S4R is shown in Figure 3.3.
3.7.2.2 Composite Bridges Modeling

A four-node shell element, type S4R, with six degrees of freedom at each node was selected to model the concrete deck slab, steel webs, steel bottom flange, and steel solid end diaphragms. A three-dimensional two-node beam element, type B31H, was chosen to model the top steel flanges, cross-bracing, and top chord.

3.7.3

Boundary Conditions

One of the most important factors for the success of the structural analysis is the proper characterization of the boundary conditions of the structural system. Condition of the columns or abutments at the support point must be examined and properly implemented into the structural analysis model. This can be accomplished via several means based on different engineering assumptions. For the static analysis, it is common to use a simple representation of support (e.g., fixed, pinned, roller) without characterizations of the soil/foundation stiffness. In modeling the

33

bridge supports in this study, the lower nodes of the web ends were restrained against translation in such way to simulate temperature-free bridge superstructure. For single span bridge: at the right end of the bridge, one of the middle supports was restrained · against all possible translations (vertical, lateral, and longitudinal), and at the other end of the bridge (left end), one of the middle supports was restrained against vertical and lateral translations. However, the rest of supports at both ends were restrained only against vertical translations. For double spans bridge: at both right and left ends, one of the middle supports was restrained against vertical and lateral translations, and at the inner supports, one of the middle supports was restrained against all possible translations (vertical, lateral, and longitudinal). However, the rest of the bridge supports were restrained only against vertical translations. Figures 3.5 and 3.6 show typical boundary conditions for single and double-span bridges respectively. The multi-point constraint option in the ABAQUS software, type BEAM, which allows constraint between different degrees of freedom, was used between the shell nodes of the concrete deck slab and the beam element nodes of the steel top flanges. This multi-point constraint option insures full interaction between the concrete deck slab and steel I-girder or box girder, thus modeling the presence of the shear connectors. A detailed diagram of the beam element B31H is shown in Figure 3.4. Appendices A & B show samples of ABAQUS input files used in this study.

3.7.4

Loads
During the bridge design process, computer models are used to evaluate brid~ structures

for various service loads, such as traffic, wind, thermal, construction, and other service loads. These service loads can be represented by a series of static load cases applied to the structural 34

model. For the purpose of comparing the results of straight bridge with curved one, a uniformly distributed load of 10 kN/m was applied to the bridge models analyzed in this study.
2

4.1 General
This
chapter~resents

an extensive parametric study, using the finite-element method, in which

The parametric study conducted herein presents the: (1) stress distribution factor; (2) deflection distribution factor; (3) reaction distribution factor; and (4) frequency distribution factor. In order to precisely determine the parameters affecting the above distribution factors, a sensitivity study was first undertaken to determine the influence of the different parameters that may affect these distribution factors, and from previous studies (Wassef, 2004 & Al-Hashimy, 2005) the following key parameters were considered: (i). A data base was generated from the parametric study, to develop empirical expressions for evaluating the distribution factors for stress, deflection, reaction, and frequency. Due to a large numbers of different bridges considered in this study, it will not be possible to demonstrate each observation for all different prototypes, and thus selection of different choices of bridge

37

configurations will be used to illustrate different comparison and observations in the parametric study.

4.2 Effect of Curvature on Distribution Factors
Curvature is considered one of the major factors that influencing the structural behavior of curved bridges. The data base generated from the parametric study was used to investigate the effect of curvature on stress, deflection, reaction, and frequency distribution factors. In this study, 240 graphs were drawn for different single & double span curved concrete & composite bridges (Box-girder, T-beam, Solid Slab, and slab on steel !-girder bridge), to study the effect of curvature on distribution factor. In these graphs, the X-axis represented the span-to-radius of curvature (L/R), the Y-axis represented the type of distribution factor, and the results of this investigation can be summarized as follows:

4.2.1 Stress Distribution in Simply Supported and Continuous Curved Bridges The effect of curvature on the stress distribution was investigated for different bridge configurations. Figure 4. 77 to 4.114 and Figure 4.197 to 218 show the stress distribution factor for different single & double span curved concrete & composite bridges (Box-girder, T-beam, Solid Slab, and slab on steel !-girder bridge), with different span length (10m to 55m), different widths (8 m, 12 m, & 16 m), and different span-to-radius of curvature ratio L/R (0.1 to 0.6), when subjected to a uniformly distributed dead load. It can be observed that as L/R ratio increases, the stress distribution factor increases. For example in Figure 4.199, as the L/R ratio increased from 0.1 to 0.6 for simply supported slab on steel !-girder bridge with 35 m span length, and 8 m width, the stress distribution factor increased from 1.63 to 5.1.

38

4.2.2 Deflection Distributions in Simply Supported and Continuous Curved Bridges

The effect of degree of curvature on deflection was examined. Figure 4.115 to 4.152 and Figure 4.219 to 4.240 present the deflection distribution factor for the same bridge configurations stated above in the stress distribution factor. It can be seen that the deflection distribution factor increases with the increase in L/R ratio. For example in Figure 4.221, as the L/R ratio increased from 0.1 to 0.6 for simply supported slab on steel !-girder bridge with 35m span length, and 8 m width , the stress distribution factor increased from 1.28 to 7.2. Moreover, it can be observed that the concrete and composite box-girder bridges have better resistance to deflection than the steel !-girder and concrete T-bearp bridges.
4.2.3 Reaction Distributions in Simply Supported and Continuous Curved Bridges

The effect of degree of curvature on reaction was examined in this parametric study. Figure 4.39 to 4.76 and Figure 4.175 to 4.196 show the reaction distribution factor (RDF) for the bridge configurations mentioned above in stress distribution factor. It can be observed that the (RDF) increases with the increase in L/R ratio. For example in Figure 4.181, as the L/R ratio increased from 0.1 to 0.6 for simply supported composite box girder bridge with 55 m span length, and 8 m width, the (RDF) increased from 1.39 to 3.42.
4.2.4 Frequency Distributions in Simply Supported and Continuous Curved Bridges

The effect of degree of curvature on Frequency was investigated. Figure 4.1 to 4.38 and Figure 4.153 to 4.174 show the frequency distribution factor (FDF) for the bridge configurations stated above in stress distribution factor. It can be seen that the (FDF) decreases with the increase in L/R ratio. For example in Figure 4.155, as the L/R ratio increased from 0.1 to 0.6 for simply

39

supported composite slab on steel I-girder bridge with 35 m span length, and 16 m width , the (FDF) decreased from 0.92 to 0.42.

4.3 Development of New Distribution Factor Equations for Curved Bridges
As stated earlier in chapter I, one of the objectives of this study was to develop empirical expressions to evaluate the stress, deflection, reaction, and frequency distribution factor. From the result of the parametric study, new empirical equations were developed for different single & double span curved concrete & composite bridges (Box-girder, T-beam, Solid Slab, and slab on ~ steel I-girder bridge curved bridges) to evaluate these parameters. The general equation for the distribution factor takes the following form:

Where DF: is the distribution factor (stress, deflection, reaction, and frequency) L : is the bridge span length in meters, R : is the radius of curvature, B : is the bridge width in meters,
a, b, c, d, and e are equation variables.

The least square method was used to develop the above equation, by minimizing the square of the difference between the distribution factors obtained from the FEA and those from the proposed equations. The Excel solver was then used to obtain the optimal solution and consequently the equation variables a, b, c, d, and e were determined The developed expressions were extended to establish more reliable expressions for curvature limitations to treat a horizontally curved bridge as a straight one. The limiting values of (LIR), to ignore curvature

40

effect were determined with 5% & 10% underestimation, and these limitations were compared with those recommended by North American Bridge Codes. Tables 4.1 to 4.4 show the list of developed expressions.

CHAPTERV SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
5.1 Summary
A series of horizontally curved bridge prototypes were analyzed in this study to examine the applicability of the specified limitations of bridge curvature in North American Bridge Codes to treat a horizontally curved bridge as straight one in structural analysis and design. Bridges taken into account are single & double-span horizontally curved concrete slab bridges, concrete girder bridges, concrete box girder bridges, slab on steel I -girder bridges, and composite steel box girder bridges. The design parameters considered in this study are degree of curvature, number of spans, span length, numbers of lanes, and span-to-depth ratio. A data base was generated from the parametric study, to develop empirical expressions for evaluating the distribution factors for stress, deflection, reaction, and frequency of curved bridges.

5.2 Conclusions
Based on the results from the parametric study, the following conclusions are drawn: ( 1) Curvature is the most important parameter that affecting the structural behavior of horizontally curved bridges. Increase in the degree of curvature leads to significant increase in the stress, deflection, and reaction distribution factors, and decrease in the frequency distribution factor. (2) This study showed that the reaction distribution factor is absolutely the most critical factor for treating single-span curved concrete and steel box-girder bridges as straight

45

one, while the deflection distribution factor is the critical one for double-span concrete (cellular & multi) and steel multi-box girder bridges. (3) The stress distribution factor is the governing factor for both single and double-span curved slab on steel !-girder bridges to treat them as straight one. (4) The CHBDC significantly underestimates the following distribution factors for curved bridges to treat them as straight one: (i) The reaction distribution factor (RDF) for all concrete and steel bridges covered in this study. (ii) The stress distribution factor (SDF) for Steel !-girder, concrete T-beam, concrete multi-box girder with spans slab with spans 55 ~ L (iii)

(5) The AASHTO guide specifications (1993, 2003) underestimate the stress distribution factor (SDF) for curved slab on steel !-girder bridges to treat them as straight one. Also the 2003 guide specifications underestimate (SDF) for single-span curved steel multi-box

(7) Sets of empirical expressions for stress, deflection, reaction, and frequency distribution factors were developed for both single and double-span curved concrete and steel bridges.

5.3

Recommendations for Future Research

Further research is recommended in the following areas: 1- The study of stress, deflection, reaction, and frequency distribution factors for curved bridges to treat them as straight one, for more than two-span. 2- The effect of skew supports in stress, deflection, reaction, and frequency distribution factors for curved bridge system. 3- The effect of CHBDC truck loading conditions on the curvature limitations established in this thesis for dead loading. 4- Study of curvature limitations for non-composite steel !-girder and box girder bridges at construction state. 5- Study of curvature limitations in two-span bridges with central interior column support. 6- Investigate the CHBDC limitations for skew bridges to be treated as straight ones.
